B006UG909U|5.0|Fair Warning! Pun Intended, and if you didn't get it, just stop here...But if you did, and for some reason you haven't purchased this disc. First off get it now, and secondly; I will provide a little insight from a fan's perspective.   This review is overwhelmingly positive. I love `A Different Kind Of Truth'. I gush and go on and on about it. If your favorite Van Halen tunes are `Jump' `Dance the Night Away', or `I'll Wait' then `A Different Kind Of Truth' isn't gonna give you much reason to `Jump' for joy. Pun intended, bad as it was... However the Van Halen clan and Roth obviously know what all us diehards have always known, which is, as much as I'm sure they enjoyed having you along to propel them into the pop stratosphere where only Michael Jackson and Madonna competed with them for the top spot, you aren't the reason they are back almost thirty years later. We are. The diehards, the deep cut lovers. We prefer our Van Halen mean, synister and fun. We prefer Fair Warning or Women and Children First to 1984 where, dispite some killer tracks like `Girl Gone Bad', `House Of Pain' and `Drop Dead Legs', we all agreed they went soft. (Ok, admittedly I Love everything on 1984, but I'm trying to make a point here. As Dave says, let's stay focused here...)   What's truly amazing is that they didn't pander to you, (though a case can be made that, `Tattoo' was released first to appease you guys) and instead chose to reward us diehards, their true core audience, bringing back every single thing we ever loved about Van Halen. What's even more amazing is that they did it so well. It's all in there, every bombastic beat, every ridiculous trademark lick, every identifiable element of the VH we all love so dearly and kept us hoping against hope that the boys would pull their heads out of their collective asses long enough to do it one more time. That they did it so creatively is sort of mind boggling, and while they did roll out all things classic VH, they added plenty new to the equation. `Honeybabysweetiepie' is heavier than anything they've ever recorded save maybe `I'm On Fire', and it certainly doesn't sound like any other tune in the cannon, owing more to Living Colour's Stained than any other identifiable influence. We've heard Motorhead do it for four decades, but Van Halen has never laid waste to horn throwing metal stomp quite the way they do on `As Is'.   That's not to say there's nothing here for more casual fans. `The Trouble With Never' `Blood And Fire', (a song only VH could pull off with any sincerity that calls to mind that old school good vibe summer night vibe of perhaps, `Little Guitars'), and `You And Your Blues' find those poppier leanings (and the required harmonies) for those who require a little less subtlety in their hooks. But be warned, even those tunes are darker, less obvious and harder rocking than you might want, but we like `em just fine. If `She's The Woman' doesn't make you wanna shout along, then I don't suppose anything ever does. And thank the maker there's not a ballad to be found anywhere. If that didn't make you stop reading, than grab a cup of something and let's get to it. Let's talk about Van Halen's return. And what a return it is...   First. Let's get this outtatheway...   For those who seem to be wanting for Michael Anthony's voice in the background, I'd suggest giving another listen to these tunes. If I cared to I might be able to note the difference, but I certainly wouldn't classify it as any sort of drop off. The Wolf does just fine. Then again, in my humble opinion, digging VH for the harmonies is comparable to buying a Lamborghini for the low profile tires. No doubt important, and even very cool, but NOT the deciding factor in driving such a machine, and ultimately too goofy a notion to spend much time pondering. If you disagree, then you probably belong in the Van Hagar camp. I say head on back to camp now, cause there's nothing on this disc that's going to assuage your indignation. Sojust go throw on Chickenfoot III and forget all about this disc... the way we've all forgotten about any disc since 1984 with a Van Halen logo on it's cover. I would also suggest that the real reason Wolfgang is in and Michael Anthony is out is to be found in the playing. If Michael A. ever played anything remotely near the same postal code Wolf resides, I've never heard it. `China Town' and the afformentioned `The Trouble With Never', feature the kind of unison Bass/Guitar attack, dodge and parry that we heard on Roth's first full length Solo disc from Sheehan and Vai, and I simply don't believe Anthony is capable of it. Were it 1986 and he was nixed in favor of Eddies son, I might have been more insensed. Alas it isn't, and I'm not. He shall now be referred to as Wolfgang, or simply Wolf. He's earned it. He's all grow'ds up now, and `Wolfy' ain't gonna cut it. VH finally has a Bass player just as badass as it's guitarist and drummer... Besides that, who would begrudge a father wanting to play with his son in a band bearing their family crest? Espescially when the kid can flat out plaaaay. (Not buying the conspiracy theorist notion that Ed played the Bass tracks. Check out the `She's A Woman' clip from the Cafe' Wha Show, the kids got the family gene)  Not me, but I guess there are those who do. It's their loss, not Van Halen's, and not ours. That said, if a new Bassist wanted to be a great Rock player, I'd hand him this album and tell him, or her, to learn it end to end, and when they could play it cold, they'd be good to go.   Now I won't spend much time on Sammy. Comparing the two incarnations is maybe the most perfect example of `Apples and Oranges' ever observed. That said, those who weild the `Hagar is a far better singer' arguement like some sort of `Check Mate' killing stroke, have always missed, and are still missing the point completely. So was Pavaratti, but I wouldn't have wanted to hear him front Van Halen. Sammy said his piece. Airing the dirty laundry to bolster his profile, and inflate his bank account. Good for him. Hope he enjoyed the low road. In hindsight though, his story feels more like a frat boy kicking a homeless man. And seeing as Eddie has apparently gotten himself together, located his misplaced crown, and made a killer comeback. Ol' good guy Sam looks a little less like a good guy. I'm guessing we won't hear much from Sam for a while. I'm still down with Mike though. Seems like a good guy.    But enough of that. Back to the return of the kings that is, `A Different Kind Of Truth'. There are folks who seem to find some disappointment, and even comfort, that `Truth' may not replace any of the first six disc as a personal favorite. Again, I feel for them. No band has ever overcome the legendary iconic masterpieces of their best work thirty years later. Not the Stones, Not McCartney, Not Robert Plant, Not U2, Not Springsteen, Not... well you get the point. Neither has any artist created a piece that will so proudly stand right along side those Masterpieces, not the way Van Halen has. Not like this. Matter of fact, I'm not sure `Truth' isn't indeed a Masterpiece in it's own right. Hard to say, I'll have to listen to it for the next thirty years to know for sure. Furthermore it may be the most consistent and therefore best disc they've ever done. Crazy right? I know, at first it may seem so, but consider at thirteen tracks it's almost a double album by VH standards, and there's not one `throwaway'. You won't find a `Big Bad Bill', `Happy trails' ` Intruder', `1984' `Sunday Afternoon In The Park', or `Tora, Tora' padding the tracklist here. No covers. Just thirteen fully realised tracks, one just as killer as the next. Come to think of it, that may be the one thing I miss on this record... I always loved that kind of spice.   Oh settle down. I hear ya shouting `What about Tattoo!?'. Here is where I'm solidly in the minority perhaps. Side Bar please...Really? Years of songs like `Don't Tell Me What Love Can Do, The Dream Is Over, Finish What Ya Started, Right Now for god's sake... And Tattoo does nothing for ya? Really? Can you see my Deniro face over here? Well to my ear, `Tattoo' is simply that hooky, timely, poppier,  Van Halen perfectly reintroducing VH to the masses. We could argue the merits of that approach, but frankly it did everything it was supposed to do. Honestly, I dig it...not that you care, and rest assured I'm not concerned that you don't, but it is heaven to my ears. Catchy, and yeah, pretty fitting in this day and age. I find it hard to believe that so many missed the sentiment with, ya know, everybody getting all tatt'd up. I think it's a really smart take on a cultural phenomenon, with a kill groove to boot, and miles above frickin' `Jump'... but yeah, I dug `Jump' too. Which only has nostagligia and our innocense to give it an edge...`Mousewife to momshell in the time it took to get that new tattoo'...Don't look now, but somehow Dave snuck poignancy into the mix, and hits the nail on the damn head. If you don't get it, you will someday. Take my word for it. Side Bar done...   I've never had a problem with acts reaching broader audiences with easier digested cuts. I like seeing shows in arenas. It's an experience like nothing else, and only an option for acts that lots of folks wanna buy tickets for. I like small intimate shows too, and see a lot of them as most acts dear to my heart now play places that hold a thousand  people. But I hope I never see VH there. If it takes a `Tattoo` or `Jump' to assure they'll be on the big stage with the big sound, I'm down. Why do you think Dave Grohl is recording warmed over nonsense like `Learning to Walk'? But did you see that last Tour? Worth a little obviousness huh? One of the best shows I've seen...   So how about the meatier offerings...well, that's everything else on this disc. If you love the barely controlled,  yell it out, boogie if you can keep up romp of `Hot For Teacher' `Truth' is a testamentthat there's plenty more where that came from. `As Is' simply blows off the doors, and you might think twice about playing it anywhere near glass. `China Town' jumps you in, welcoming you to one of those communities Dave was visiting when he wrote `Mean Street'. Meanwhile `Outta Space', while goofy in the way anything is that ties itself to sci-fi imagery,  revs it up like they did on `Atomic Punk', not particularly melodic, or tuneful. But allowing the VH men to let lose like only they can. Answering the question, `What do you get when you add more Van Halen?', the answer of course... `More Van Halen', and when it's THIS Van Halen, that ain't a bad thing. When Dave tells the visiting whatever to `Go home, the earth is full' , don't be surprised if you find yourself mentally hollering `Yeah Mother F*&^%ers!'...`Bullethead' is the defintion of Metal Cool. Remeber when Metal was cool? Best song about road rage ever! `Buh buh buh buh buh buh bullethead'. Come On! I won't dissect everything. Suffice it to say If you can't get down with those tunes. Buddy, you can't get down.   Yeah, they pulled out old demos for half the albums tunes. That anyone would take exception to that is beyond my comprehension, which admittedly may not be what it once was. After hearing the results I wish more gereatric acts would consider the tactic, and you probably will too. Or maybe the semantics just distract you from enjoying the end result. Do you prefer newer Aerosmith to their classic 70's output? Hmm. It ultimately says more about you than the band if you do, and the same could be said about `Truth'. Case in point, the Stones last great record, `Tattoo You' was made up of leftovers cobled together from their 70's output. Just sayin'. In the end, I couldn't care less how these thirteen tunes were born, or when, just as long as I have `em to enjoy. And oh yeah... It totally worked.   Some tunes sound like brothers to classics. `Big River' had to have came to Eddie in the same wave of inspiration as `Runnin' With The Devil'. I got no problem with that, and if you do, again, it says more about you than them. Namely you don't love VH the way we do. Which is ok with me, but why are you still reading this? Your mind was made up already.... `Beats Workin' mines the same vein that yielded, `So This Is Love' and perhaps even their it's so much better than the original I've never listened to the original again version of `Where Have All The Good Times Gone' and is the perfect closer to the disc.   If you love the VH for the drumming, I sure do, then you won't be disappointed either. Alex is present and accounted for, in a way he hasn't been for almost three decades now. I mean, I could go on and on, pointing out time spots to check out in every single tune for examples of his supreme badassadry (Now a word), but this review is too long by far already so I won't. (OK, I'll give you one ... Head for the two minute mark in `China Town' listen to that prechorus that launches Eddies Solo. Awesome....) Rest assured, he sounds as nimble and powerfully bombastic as he ever has. Which, at his age, is simply almost uncomprehendable. Just as head scratching as Jagger careening around the stage for two and a half hours on the last Stones tour. It just doesn't make sense. His limb independance, hang tight while us drummers talk for a moment, is astounding and effortless just like you remember, and how he can play this tastefully while creating so much of a hurricane defies all logic. Listen to his Ride work during that Eddie Solo in `China Town'. Now go try it...See? His playing on `Truth' reminds me why I kept picking up those sticks when I was still light years away from being able to use `em the way he does. I've gotten a light year or two closer in the decades since, but he is still just flat out inspiring. I feel like I'm twelve again, sitting at the foot of the Master. If he's underrated I don't know how. Only Bonham cast a shadow as long as his in the Rock and Roll Universe.   As for Eddie? Eddie is still the king, and he brings all the tools in his considerable belt to this fight. For me, and for a whole bunch of folks, there's Eddie, and then there's everyone else. (  I might argue that about Van Halen as a band if pressed, but then you'd think I had no perspective. Trust me, I love a bunch of other bands) Yep, Vai may be techinically better, Satch may be more fluid. What has always set Eddie apart isn't his soloing, which people will still be dissecting at the end of the world be it in ten months or ten thousand years, or even the cacauphony of random sounds he can conjure up at any and every moment. It's his ability to do it within the context of a song and somehow make it oh so listenable and enjoyable. Lyrical even.There's no logical reason for his playing to be so pleasing to the ear. It could easily sound like nails on a chalkboard, and yet it never does. It's a bit of a universal mystery really. It's all on display here. Some folks say he hasn't moved the ball forward with `Truth' to which I would respond; when you run it back for a touchdown, the only thing left is to do it again. No, he doesn't have the `Never heard this before' angle to attack from anymore, but that's because he already hit us from every angle. If this somehow takes away from your enjoymeant of hearing him weild that arsenal one more time, I simply don't know what to tell ya. Again, `your loss' is the only thing that springs to mind. That said, there are certainly a few new tricks to be found, and a few different tones than have been heard in the catalog so far. When he does pull out signature riffage, he uses it in different places than you'd expect. Revealing how much thought went into this baby. When he gives a nod to what passed for rock guitar in the last twenty years , like on `Honeybabysweetiedoll', you can hear Nu-Metal dorks all over the world saying, `Damnit, that's how you're supposed to do it' ... It's all in the fingers, and his areaparently no worse for wear and tear. Astounding....(But if your looking for someone to reinvent the wheel, may I suggest Radiohead. A band I dig for sure. Have fun with that) Eddie already reinvented the wheel, and built the car to put it on. How many times does any one man do that in a lifetime. If this is your point of contention, I suspect you simply have the bar too high on everything in life. You must be very disappointed in ... well everything I guess.   But let's face it. The reason this disc got the attention it has is because of the return of Roth. Not to say that he's the reason it's as good as it is. Or maybe to say exactly that. Consider this. The Hagar years were full of great playing by the brothers Van Halen... the problem was Sammy. As genericly solid as his singing was, no better than an American Idol contestant if only for being so unmemorable, it was his writing that was the problem, along with his drunk guy singing kareokee delivery. Aww Shucks only goes so far... He simply lacks the cleverness to turn a decent phrase. That is what sets song lyrics apart from any random converstion. Example; `How do you know when it's love? I don't know, but it last forever', and of course `Hey Baby', which he has got to give credit for to the Big Bopper. He simply lacked the lyrical mustard, or delivery to throw a saddle on the wild mustang that is Van Halen's sound without getting thrown off. Roth on the other hand can ride that beast the full eight seconds...and yeah, that's certainly a huge factor in the success of this effort.   Dave and Eddie are the Bullets and the Gun, switching roles as to which depending on where we are in the song. Which one kills you kind of misses the point. Roth sings, wether you call it that or not, with the attitude, energy and spontanuity (the oops missed it last `Tattoo' on that last verse just kills me. And the gri-gri of him asking for more of him in the headphones in the breakdown of `As Is', that's the stuff!) that perfectly matches Eddie's genius, and he still has plenty of all of the above. Like a translator for visiting royalty, he clarifies, and personifies what they are saying musically the way that apparently only he can. Say what you will, something about him and Ed's chemistry sparks the best in everyone. The meaning ain't the point. The sentiment is.    `Stay Frosty' might be the best example of pure Rothism ever, his particular brand of carny act, the way `Ice Cream Man' was until now. And yep, they are different versions of the same song, but nostalgia aside, `Stay Frosty' might be better. ..And yes, it was intentional. `Ice Cream Man'....`Stay Frosty'... Get It? Write it down... try to keep up. `Don't want `em to get your goat? Don't show `em where it's hid.', then soon after, `Here, you can use my hand, I won't look...Uuuh Ooh'..Elsewhere, `There's a trick to christian values, and it's compulsory'  and later, `Far and wide, far as you ramble, trust in allah, but tie up your camel'. Hehehehe.. ahhh. Good stuff. Anyway, if you take no pleasure from hearing that trick one more time, which is precisely the second time they have pulled it out, then just stop. Forget it, you don't like VH...move on. The rest of us, we're gonna go berzerk when the band fires in on that bad boy boogie.. ...If you love VAN HALEN... You're gonna love this. If you don't, you won't... If your disappointed. My lord, what more did you want? What could you possibly have been expecting, and how could you possibly have expected it?!  And why are you so sad?   The lyrical gems are all over `Truth', `Listen to a seashell, you can hear the sea. Listen to a beer mug, that river belongs to me'- `God is love, but get it in writing'- `With a Chevy for my summer home, let's get the party started. Looks like the city towed my other apartment'- `God must love the lazy, shiftless and the crazy, he put so many in line' - `Honeybaby my hearts aflame I'm all...And you're to blame'. Don't know logically what all that means, but my heart knows and beats a little faster like an unborn baby responding to it's father's voice in the womb. `I say love `em all, let cupid sort `em out'. How has nobody said that before?!   Where was I? Oh yeah, Roth...Yep, or rather nope, his range ain't what it used to be, and the train whistle is nowhere to be heard... although how he ever did that is still fun to ponder, but it's hardly a deal breaker, at least not for me, and if you're still reading, probably not for you. `She's the Woman' has all the swaggar and bravado needed to make you throw your hand in the air and shout along, unless your the one I was talking about way up at the top of this near monstrous novel of a review. And the real appeal of Roth, what was missing without him, is found heading into that song's second chorus where he informs us that `the song aint dirty, it's really just the way we sing it', again, clever in case you forgot what that sounds like, then in the background he invokes the Muppet's Animal questioning first `Sing it?', then deciding YES, shouting `SING IT'. He also deserves a bit more credit than he's given. He's not just clever, he's actually smarter than the average bear. I've read where some didn't get the lyrics on the next tune I'm talking about (To you I say go do some research. The roots of all this stuff are a hundred years old, and you've missed a bunch) `You and Your Blues', where he thoughtfully quotes classic Blues songs and lines to illustrate that he's a pretty happy dude, all fed up with his girl's inability to look on the damn bright side before hollering `Woman, you suffer from a color'... Blues...Get it? If the song doesn't strike a chord with you, I envy, and sort of hate you, but for lots of men out there, and I imagine some ladies, the sentiment is gonna hit home. Nice of him to get it off our chest. I'll go back to `As Is' to really drive the point home. As the tune fades out with Eddie sliding around the neck like a rhythmic serpent over Alex's gallop and Wolf's low end machine-gun cannonballs, Roth, seemingly at a loss for words after all that pure rock and roll exhilaration, simply sings, and I quote, `La La La La, La La La Laaaa La', and for whatever reason only the gods know, it makes perfect sense.That kind of sums it all up... `As he states after the first chorus, `That's how it go'. This is what Roth brings to the table. Really the only comparison you can make with his personna is the Joker, specifically. The Dark Knight's Heath Ledger portrayal. Throughout the disc, Roth makes evident his lust for life, even if he's now taken a punch or two, the likes of which he hadn't yet while recording the first six discs. The weariness hasn't dulled him though. If anything it only heightens his sense of `Get it while you can' bravado. On the more menacing tunes it's not hard to imagine him asking us all as a collective, `Why so serious', and then slashing us wide open with a switchblade. Take another listen to that halftime breakdown in `The Trouble With Never'. I mentioned earlier...   Look, that kind of stuff either does it for you or it doesn't. Somewhere along the line, seems the world forgot that rock and roll was meant to be fun. Hip Hop/Rap never forgot, and I'm pretty sure that is what pulled more recent generations to it. Roth has come to reclaim his territory, and despite his aging chords he manages to do just that.   Hopefully, no one is interested in deciding who deserves more credit. The fact is, Van Halen is another example of the whole being greater than the sum of it's parts, despite those considerably talented parts. It's all of them - together, that makes the magic happen. No point in jealousy, or whatever it was that drove them apart, ruining what they are - together. Only Roth gives a frame of reference for what Eddie does, and without the Van Halens providing the context, Roth is just a lunatic spewing nonsense. So here's hoping they get together at least one more time to give us another collection of songs. More old ideas, new ones, whatever, as long as it's the real Van Halen, I'll wait.   Sorry...couldn't resist.    Another real testament to the time and focus they brought to this project are the arrangements. More Van Halen's time than Roth's I'm guessing, but who knows? They're surprising but not overthought. Making it sound and, more importantly, feel like VH is a living beast that you somehow managed to jump on back of for a few minutes as it charged swayed and stopped dead, only to take a step or two before taking off again. The haftime section in `Trouble With Never' is but one example, and a mild one. Other tunes showcase changes in time signature and jazz scales that add to the uniqueness of those tunes and make a case that if anything, they're actually better musicians now. The songs are cinematic. But then the best VH always was. `Tattoo' is indeed the most conventional arrangement to be found. As are most Radio minded tunes, but nothing else follows the standard formula of song construction. Oh, it's not Prog Rock by any means, but they take care to throw in curves and U turns that are sure to reward as time goes on. Guess what I'm trying to say is, these are well crafted songs that reward with repeated listenings, but what fun is that?   To summarize I will quote the beloved portrayal of a musical legend on film. `The Legend of Dewey Cox'.  Taken from the the scene where old Dewey hangs with the Beatles. Not sure which one said it first, Paul or John... but it says it all.   `Ggrrrllleeaat Recooorrd'   That's more than I hoped for...   It's a different kind of truth, but it's the truth nonetheless, and the truth will set you free, the way only Van Halen ever could!

B004GHYCKW|5.0|I've known about PJ Harvey for a long time, but never have I taken to time to listen to her music.  I'm a Nick Cave fan though, so her duet and other musical contributions to him are familiar to me.  Seeing this album on a few 10 Best lists and her winning The Mercury Prize for this was enough to get my attention.  This album has become an instant favorite of mine and I would definitely agree it is a cohesive album worth listening to as a whole work.  In this day and age, it's a rarity to find a full album you can say is a work of art.  This is one of them.  My only complaint is tracks 10 and 11, while good songs, somehow break up the incredible pace and I feel the need to usually skip them.  Still, I'm giving 5 stars because even with this one gripe, this is still an AMAZING record.  Now I have a lot more PJ Harvey to explore.  Highly recommended.
B006UG90RM|5.0|I absolutely love this album.  I was a little worried about the new album when I heard Tattoo, but this is one awesome album.  First of all, it's heavy, like Women and Children First heavy, which I love.  However, it almost feels like this is the next step from 1984 without the keyboards.  Eddie is on fire on this album, his solos are wild and flawless while Alex pounds through songs like John Bonham (he's IMO, the best ROCK drummer since Bonham, his sound, his playing, I love it).  The bass is higher in the mix than other VH albums which is a good thing and Wolf sounds like a very competent player and does a fine job replacing Michael Anthony (though I wish his vocals were still there).  DLR can't yelp and reach some of the high notes like he used to, but he was never really a great singer anyway, he just had the \\"it\\"  factor which he once again brings to the table.  Roth brings that swagger we've been missing with the other singers.  The production is excellent on the album too.  It's not over-compressed like most songs today. 0062073567|4.0|I've never been a huge fan of the \\"cozy\\" mystery, but in my rare ventures into that territory, I like to go with Agatha Christie, one of the biggest names in the genre.  What little I knew of The Murder of Roger Ackroyd was it somehow controversial, which to me was a virtue.  Having now read the book, I can see where the controversy was at the time, but really, it is more cutting edge in a way I can't really discuss without spoiling things.  It is not, I hope, too much of a spoiler to say that this book has a character named Roger Ackroyd and he is indeed murdered.  Besides the normal police, the private investigator Hercule Poirot is called in to investigate.  Ackroyd was a wealthy but often miserly man, and his murder brings forth a number of suspects, all with their own motives.  The most suspicious in the bunch is his stepson Ralph Paton, who's disappeared shortly after the killing and stands to inherit a lot of money.  Of course, in most mysteries, the obvious suspect is rarely the actual culprit.  Poirot will slowly piece things together.  Narrating the tale, almost as a Watson to the Holmes-like Poirot, is the local doctor, James Sheppard.  Sheppard's nosy sister Caroline would serve as a precusor to Miss Marple, Christie's second most memorable character.  The most memorable is Poirot, who is featured in nearly half of Christie's 80 books (Marple is in only a dozen or so).  Like Sherlock Holmes, he is a brilliant but egotistical man with his share of affectations.  And like many detectives of the era (including more hard-boiled sorts like Philip Marlowe), he is essentially an outsider; he will never get really involved in the other characters' lives and at the end of the novel, he will no doubt leave them with genial ease.  This is a good book and would serve well as an introduction to both Christie and Poirot.  The bits of cleverness you may notice may seem not surprising now, but when this book was released, they were more special.  Even if you're not a big cozy mystery fan, this book still serves well for those who want to know the history of the mystery genre.

I mean, LOOK at all those five star reviews.<br /><br />For starters, despite Mistborn being touted by some as a \\"the bad guy wins\\" book, it is actually as generic as almost any other book using a dark lord. The only difference in this book is he isn't trying to reclaim power, only maintain it. Now, don't get me wrong the premise could be good fun, but the way it is conveyed is way too painful to truly enjoy.<br /><br />Now before I rip into the book, I should say It wasn't so bad that I couldn't finish, only that I had to skim it during some of the tedious middle portions. Also, I was actually interested to learn about some of the mysteries- though that just made it more of a slap in the face when no effort is made to give them a resolution.<br /><br />The characters in the series are where I suppose, many of the problems ultimately stem from. We have Vin and Kelsier as the main characters. Despite being the most fleshed out, Vin was never ever slightly likable with her plethora of inner monologues and brother issues. Kelsier fared better, but still felt pretty generic and had too many lines and moments evidently made to force the reader to like him. The rest of the characters are vague, scarcely fleshed out shadows, with single attributes that define them. Unfortunately this is even the case for the lord ruler. He gives advice on showing rather than telling, and I expected quality closer to GRRM's work based on that. Yet he has a terrible habit of stating the obvious and never implying things. Sometimes it is needed, because the character reaction is so unbelievable you need to hear it from them, other times it is obvious but stated anyways.<br /><br />The pacing is also utterly terrible. Brandon clearly wanted to make a story about an epic uprising, but wasn't too clear on how to get to the actual uprising. We meet the characters and get some description of the world, before the plethora of training sections. The whole noble parties could have been fun, but dialogue and characterization is just so weak that everyone came across as copy pastes of stereotypes, especially Kliss who we meet for a second, then later on is abruptly an informer(which also doesn't go anywhere).<br /><br />Once the actual uprising occurs the pace goes from slow to wildly erratic, with random bursts of heroism, false flag deaths, sacrifices etc. But it is all cramped in such a close section, and glued together so poorly, the events are jagged and odd. The occurrences themselves are also very unbelievable, such as the event which sets off the uprising- which was very cliche, and spelled out immediately in the next chapter.<br /><br />All in all this book was just not there. Nothing felt truly inspired. Did I think it might have had potential? Sure, before there was any real dialogue. Were some parts cool? Well, not really but the fighting was interesting the first time. Would I recommend this? Absolutely not, unless your a fan of new magic systems in a bland setting.it is obvious but stated anyways.<br /><br />The pacing is also utterly terrible. Both of them were tremendously popular during their time, but they obviously ended up on someone's junk pile.<br /><br />\\"Boston\\" and \\"Frampton Comes Alive\\" were pretty much the precursors of arena and glam rock, and inadvertently caused a backlash from the rock underground by the emergence of punk bands like the Ramones, Husker Du, the Dead Boys and so on.<br /><br />Although huge, huge megahits, neither \\"Boston,\\" or \\"Frampton Comes Alive,\\" found their way onto Rolling Stone's \\"500 Greatest Albums\\" list.<br /><br />There's a reason for that.<br /><br />On one hand, \\"Frampton Comes Alive\\" was just a rehash of about four or five albums worth of studio recordings. It wasn't bad, it was just overdone, like a burnt weenie. On the other hand, \\"Boston\\" was a collection of rock cliches that were obsolete on arrival. It some ways, it was like \\"The 1965 Beach Boys meet the 1969 Led Zepplin\\".<br /><br />Record companies manufacture musical junk to sell to young girls, not old guys in their fifties or sixties who would rather listen to John Coltrane, Bob Dylan or Bob Mould.  Old guys don't have the money to throw around at LP's, CD's or MP3 downloads anyway. Young girls are a much better market than old guys. They spend more money.<br /><br />Since they wanted to be successful, I'm sure that both Tom Scholz and Peter Frampton were well aware of this.  And when you're writing for such a commercial target audience, artistry goes right out the window.  That's why neither \\"Boston,\\" or \\"Frampton Comes Alive,\\" made Rolling Stone's top 500 list.  Although, to be fair, if the editors of Rolling Stone were more conservative in their criteria, either album could have easily been somewhere in there.<br /><br />Popular culture ultimately ends up in the garbage, or in bins at thrift stores. If you're spending any more than a few dollars to see any sort of live entertainment, you're paying too much. Both \\"Boston\\" and \\"Frampton Comes Alive\\" were the precursors of the junk culture we now have and were a real heads up for the money-hungry execs in the entertainment industry.<br /><br />Popular culture is common. Popular culture is aimed at those who are most susceptible to bend and least likely to be able to fend against it. Popular culture's only purpose is to make money for those at the top of the pyramid, to keep \\"the elite\\" in intoxicants and pay for megamansions and yachts.<br /><br />To be fair, if it wasn't \\"Boston\\" and \\"Frampton Comes Alive\\", it would have been something else. In place of \\"The Beach Boys meet Led Zepplin\\" there could have been \\"The Dave Clark Five meets Grand Funk Railroad\\", That's why I gave this CD a mediocre rating, and not just one star. It isn't bad, but it sure ain't art.<br /><br />Most people are going to hate what I've just said.  For the record, I didn't like \\"Lord of the Rings,\\" and I don't like the music of Jon Bon Jovi either. I don't go to McDonald's or Burger King, and I think the cost of a ticket to an NBA game is obscene.<br /><br />In 1968, tickets to see Jimi Hendrix were less than $5.00 for good seats. In 2012, good seats for any live event are commonly $500.00 and upwards. All this just to see aging rock stars or unproven, overpaid athletes. Apparently, where the money goes to the event-goers is unimportant.<br /><br />My advice? Pay a few bucks to see your musical acts in a live local venue other than in an arena. Go to a minor league game, or a high school football game. Support your local library as much as possible.  Buy stuff from amazon.com and stream video from the internet, but support cable TV as little as possible. Buy it, you won't regret it.

Apparently, men beating each other to a pulp wasn't enough carnage for fight fans, facilitating the transition into full-sized Rock'em Sock'em Robots, although I can imagine that doctors who pointed to boxing as a source of brain damage and early-onset Parkinson's Disease would likely be pleased by such a change.<br /><br />Charlie is forced to change with the sport he loves, training bots to battle in underground battles held at rodeos, factories, and condemned zoos. Charlie longs for a shot in the World Robot Boxing league, but his arrogance has left him with a string of destroyed bots and owing thousands of dollars that he can't pay back.<br /><br />Forced to attend a custody hearing for his estranged 11 year old son Max (newcomer Dakota Goyo), Charlie talks Max's wealthy uncle into paying him to take care of Max for the summer. A huge robot fight fan, Max quickly takes an interest in his father's profession, much to Charlie's chagrin. When Charlie and Max break into a junkyard in search of replacement parts for a broken bot, Max discovers Atom, a discarded sparring bot that was built to endure a lot of punishment but not dish much out. Max insists on using Atom to get into the robot boxing world, and Charlie grudgingly agrees to train Atom to fight like a human boxer. The once-contentious father and son bond as Charlie's boxing savvy and Max's unwavering faith in Atom (as well as Max's amusing dance moves) lead to Atom's rise through the underground ranks, catching the eye of the WRB. And after Atom impressively defeats a WRB contender, Max publicly issues a challenge to Zeus, the reigning undefeated champ, leading to a \\"David vs. Goliath\\" showdown for all the metal marbles (or would that be ball bearings?).<br /><br />Also populating this film are Lost's Evangeline Lilly, essentially filling out the \\"Adrian role\\" as Bailey Tallet, the daughter of Charlie's deceased trainer who has turned her father's old boxing gym into a pseudo-Frankenstein's workshop for Charlie's bots. Anthony Mackie from The Hurt Locker appears as Finn, an underground promoter and one of Charlie's few \\"friends,\\" and Kevin Durand as Ricky, one of Charlie's former in-ring adversaries who Charlie owes money to after skipping out on a bot-fighting bet.<br /><br />Real Steel prescribes to all the cliches started by the Rocky films of a down-on-his luck underdog who defies the odds and gets a shot at the big time, but the film also has a little bit in common with another Sylvester Stallone film, Over The Top. Both films involve a father and his estranged son who grate on each other at first, but end up working out their abandonment issues and bond over a similar interest even as outside forces and their own clashing personalities threaten to rip them apart. Many would also find shades of the Transformers films in the \\"battling robots\\" plot, but unlike those films whose attraction lied mainly in mechanical carnage of CGI-bot-on-CGI-bot violence, Real Steel's strength lies in its human players, namely Charlie and Max, giving the film a soul. And you can't help but cheer when Charlie starts shadow boxing at ringside during the Zeus fight.

What was I waiting for?! \\"Saw\\" is the first of this amazing franchise in horror and it's a goody. It's fresh, smart and keeps you guessing. With a unique storyline complete with twists, surprises, puzzles and intense horror, \\"Saw\\" is movie that keeps you watching. As the story unfolds, you won't believe how the people are so linked together; having more in common then you can know. And you especially won't believe the ending! It is truly epic. Director James Wan and writer Leigh Whannell did a great job with this film.  In a downright disgusting bathroom, Adam (Leigh Whannell), a voyeur photographer, wakes up in a bathtub with his foot chained to a pipe. Across from him, foot also chained to a pipe is Lawrence Gordon (Cary Elwes), a doctor. In the middle of the room lies the body of a man, a gun clutched in one hand and a tape player in the other. Adam and Lawrence each find a tape in their pocket and upon playing them, are met with the creepy recorded voice giving them the staggering situation: Lawrence must kill Adam by 6:00 or his wife (Monica Potter) and daughter (Makenzie Vega) will die and Adam will either watch himself die or do something about it. The voice is that of the \\"Jigsaw Killer,\\" a psychopath who kidnaps people he feels are ungrateful and undeserving of their lives and hurls them into the thralls of bloody, mind-numbing, torturous, life-threatening games of traps, where they must find new appreciation for their lives by facing death. Adam and Lawrence are the newest pawns in Jigsaw's twisted charade, and clues to their \\"game\\" are around the bathroom.  We first learn about Jigsaw through Lawrence's recollections of three other grisly \\"games\\" that were conducted. There's Paul, the suicidal man who comes face to face with razor wire. There's Mark, a man notorious for faking illness who finds himselfcovered in a flammable substance and needing a candle to help find a safe combination. Then there's Amanda, the drug addict who wakes up with a reverse bear trap hooked up to her head and jaws. To unlock it, she needs the key from inside the stomach of her dead cellmate. There is also one other Jigsaw trap that happens later on in the movie involving a man named Jeff. Jeff is sitting in a chair, his neck braced in place. A button is stepped on, which activates two drills coming at Jeff; one on each side of his head. According to Jigsaw, he is giving Jeff's life a purpose.  But \\"Saw\\" also focuses around the detectives investigating the Jigsaw murders; detectives Steven Sing (Ken Leung) and detective David Tapp (Danny Glover). We see flashbacks of Tapp and Sing investigating Jigsaw traps, interviewing a possible suspect and talking with a Jigsaw survivor. Both are very committed to the Jigsaw case but Tapp becomes obsessed with it after an unfortunate incident. This obsession will ultimately lead him to someone else who is just as obsessed and desperate, but also dangerous.  As the movie progresses, the intensity heightens as time ticks away for Adam and Lawrence. I've seen some reviews saying that the acting is either bad, or just \\"okay.\\" I for one, actually liked the acting, especially from Cary Elwes as the drama builds. I thought he did a great job displaying the intense pain, desperation, anger and feeling of helplessness. I really got into the emotion he brings to the film. And you can feel the psychological madness within him as he prepares to make and conducts some dramatic decisions. Leigh Whannell brings a little levity to the movie as Adam. His sarcastic wit brings some choice phrases to the film amongst the horror. He has a calmness, yet you feel his helplessness as well. Monica Potter and Makenzie Vega aren't bad for acting either as they are held hostage in their home. And then there's Danny Glover, playing a pivotal role as the obsessed former detective. He does a great job with some thrilling scenes in the film.  \\"Saw\\" is a great horror movie will leave you questioning things every minute. Though not as gory as the other Saw movies, I still wouldn't recommend it for the faint of heart. The special effects are pretty good for a low-budget film. It's intense, disturbing and horrific; the way a good horror movie should be. Horror fans won't be disappointed. If you haven't seen \\"Saw\\" yet, you're missing out. Get involved in this awesomely sick and twisted horror franchise and start with the movie that began the saga.

1441711643|4.0|Reading The day of the Jackal is a genuine pleasure in the tell-me-a-story, invest-me-in-rich-characters, transport-and-titillate-me a little and-spin-a-rich-yarn sense.<br /><br />This story succeeds with a nuance rarely seen in today's rapid fire style action thriller.  And does it ever succeed!  Bear in mind that to enjoy this you're expected to soak in details of life in Europe in the early 1960'---to be transported back to days when it took 30 minutes for a long distance call to connect.  The action unfolds at a normal human pace, unaided by strident and intrusive technology.  People have to pause their action, often for hours or even a day at a time, to wait for fresh information to start flowing again and set the next act in motion.<br /><br />The rotary phone pacing means you have time to ponder past events and look ahead to what's ahead in the next pages.  It also adds tremendously to the experience as you become engaged in the slow, methodical hunt for the Jackal and concurrently in the evasive tactical maneuvers of the Jackal to elude his pursuers.<br /><br />Forsyth provides his readers with brief, teasing looks over the plot horizon and then restrains you as circumstances slowly unwind to deliver surprising twists and turns from his major characters--the slick and instinctive Jackal playing mouse to plodding, rumpled, but quietly cerebral Lebel.  To enjoy this fully you have to be willing to forgo explosive immediate gratification for a slower gratification made so much sweeter by it's consistently textured delivery.<br /><br />The ending satisfies but it's the rich and nuanced journey that will stick with you when you lay the book down (or turn the audiobook off).<br /><br />So if you're willing trade down from warp drive to 1960's style mach drive you'll relish the outcome of this literary experience.

B005LAIGPA|5.0|When friends have a falling out, it's best for all parties involved - including unwitting bystanders - that they don't have destructive superhuman abilities. Because the collateral damage off that spat is wicked crazy. CHRONICLE is one of them \\"found footage\\" films, the narrative unfolding pseudo-documentary-style mainly thru video cameras employed by the characters. Other recording devices - cell phone & traffic cameras and security feeds and tools brought to bear by breaking news media coverage - come into play later on in the tremendous third act.<br /><br />CHRONICLE centers around the strange thing what befalls three high school seniors: the popular and charismatic Steve (Michael B. Jordan); the smart and just a bit pretentious Matt (Alex Russell); and Andrew (Dane DeHaan), the weirdo loner burdened with the dying mom and abusive dad. It's Andrew who opts to escape his disturbing home life by throwing himself into his handycam hobby. As escapism he films everything in sight. He takes his video recorder everywhere. While attending a rave, Steve, Matt, and Andrew are diverted by a deep hole in the ground in the nearby woods. What they eventually stumble across somehow imbues them with super powers. Soon Steve, Matt, and Andrew are experimenting (read: goofing around) with telekinesis and learning how to fly (which is just another extension of telekinesis). Of course, there are the requisite nose bleeds.<br /><br />Andrew demonstrates a natural affinity for his newfound talents. They develop more quickly than do Matt's or Steve's. So at least he has that to feel good about. But there's a disconnect with the troubled Andrew. The family home melodrama exacerbates his sense of alienation. Still, their mutual powers draw the trio closer together. They begin to hang out a lot more.<br /><br />There's an organic flow to the story, or during the first two thirds anyway. The script allows the actors to sound off with believable dialogue. The three leads are natural in their roles; there's an authentic vibe. I like to think that the stuff they get up to initially, in exploring their powers, are stuff that normal teenagers would think up. Their antics in the supermarket are pretty hilarious, even though that was a mean thing they did, spooking the little girl. Me, I probably won't be able to help abusing my powers a little bit. Steve, Matt, and Andrew apply theirs to have fun and impress other kids (by, say, staging a magic show or showing off while playing the quarters beer game). Matt is the most level-headed of the bunch. He at least seems aware of the necessity of keeping their secret. He tries to lay down some ground rules, like not using their abilities on living things or using them when they're angry. It's worrying that the much bullied Andrew doesn't see the point of these rules.<br /><br />Most of the film's first half is so lighthearted and goofy that it's all the more impactful when things finally get serious. One of the three begins to subscribe to that old \\"survival of the fittest\\" way of thinking. He begins to regard himself as the ultimate predator and mere humans as disposable insects. The other two try to talk sense into him, but, by that stage, we're all just pretty much waiting for the inevitable downspiral into fighty fight.<br /><br />I happened to really like CLOVERFIELD, a recent \\"found footage\\" monster film (I know a lot of folks didn't). I think that CHRONICLE shares CLOVERFIELD'S sense of immediacy. Only, this time, you get a feel for what it may really be like to be caught on ground zero when ridiculously powerful metahumans furiously duke it out. One famous landmark in Seattle gets a shellacking.<br /><br />Did you think the film needed to delve more into the crystalline object in the underground cavern which (it's assumed) bestowed paranormal talents on them kids? I don't think so. I even liked that the story dropped it. The kids make an attempt to revisit the hole in the ground, but things don't pan out,and they shrug it off. And while the introduction of the girl who records with her own camera for her blog may be a convenient plot device, she does ground one of the characters. I like that there's a thru line to the story, that ***SPOILERS NOW FOR THE REST OF THE SENTENCE*** the superhumans are later exposed to the world. This raises the stakes tremendously. It encompasses the frenetic third act. There are some archetypal elements that come into play in this collision between an amoral monster and his friend still trying to talk him down. And it ultimately defines the makings of a hero. The special effects are pretty amazing considering the film's low budget. The flying sequences are breathlessly entertaining. And while I enjoyed the sense of humor and the cool superhero beats, these alone wouldn't have been enough. You required those solid chunks of storytelling and character development to prop them up.and they shrug it off. 1439192812|5.0|When I woke this morning, it was to a light fluttering of snow, gently settling on the branches of the trees outside my bedroom window. Now that I am up the rate of snowfall has increased and larger, fluffy-white flakes are starting to come down in greater number. The ground is starting to get a nice coating of white and a quietness has settled over the surrounding countryside. In days of yore this may have been viewed as the action of some all-seeing sky-daddy (or mom). It may even have been ascribed to the fact that we had displeased said deity in some way or another, perhaps I would have linked it as a punishment evoked on my family for my having a heated argument with my daughter yesterday evening, after all it wasn't snowing then!<br />However, as Humanity's questioning, testing and understanding of the world has increased over the centuries, we now know that this is a complex natural phenomenon involving a precipitation pattern associated with seasonal temperature changes, weather fronts and local geographies. We were even able to know in advance and tell people that this was coming.We are even able to predict, with a reasonable amount of accuracy, the duration of this activity.<br /><br />There is no godly intervention or explanation required.<br /><br />And that is the subject of Richard Dawkins' wonderful book, The Magic of Reality, which I was sent for xmas, and have just gotten around to reading.<br /><br />This book is a truly inspirational piece of writing by Richard Dawkins<br /><br />In The Magic of Reality, Dawkins covers a huge tract of human knowledge in a logical fashion from the physical, chemical and biological sciences. Generally each chapter starts out with some mythical background, based on many world religions views from modern xtianity through to ancient Greek, Indian, Chinese, South American and Australasian aboriginal myths, to illustrate the point that is being explained. Then, in a non-confrontational way Dawkins peels away the myths and exposes what we truly know based on our current understanding through scientific analysis.<br />The breadth of subjects covered is inspiring, and I would assume that is the whole point of the book, to open our minds and inspire further investigation. How different from any religious mumbo-jumbo that is force-fed to the youth in their religious proselytizing, where Our Book must not be interpreted, and any logical questions must be dismissed with dogma!<br /><br />The take home message from The Magic of Reality is that the universe is a wonderfully complex entity about which we know a lot, but clearly not everything. Scientific thinking and theories continue to evolve but the scientific rigor for unrelenting questioning, testing and further examination leading to an understanding is key to Humankind's advancement. It is time to leave the unhelpful dogma and myths behind.<br /><br />[[ASIN:1439192812 The Magic of Reality: How We Know What's Really True]] is beautifully illustrated and I shall be passing my copy around the family to see how my children take to this logical book.<br /><br />This should be a core publication in every school library and available for all children to read.d exposes what we truly know based on our current understanding through scientific analysis.<br />The breadth of subjects covered is inspiring, and I would assume that is the whole point of the book, to open our minds and inspire further investigation. B005SFR7YE|5.0|\\"The Phantom of the Opera\\" has always been one of my absolutely favorite stories.  Its exploration of true beauty, love, sacrifice and isolation is timeless and proves why this story never grows old.  Webber's musical adaptation of Gaston Lerroux's novel is sheer beauty.  This show was my first introduction to musical theatre, so I am, of course, biased.  Nevertheless, this 25th anniversary concert of this timeless show contains some of the best renditions of the music I have ever heard.<br /><br />The Paris Opera House is besieged by mysterious and often dangerous pranks.  A shadowy figure haunts its labyrinthian halls and secret underground passageways.  When this charismatic, dangerous and ultimately tormented \\"Phantom\\" falls in love with little-known chorus girl, Christine Daae, he begins molding her into a wondrous singer.  However, because of a severe facial deformity that has alienated him even from birth, this musical genius remains hidden from his protegee.  Although he instructs Christine, he cannot allow her to see him.  Christine struggles with loneliness and grief herself.  Orphaned at an early age, her mind is filled with the childhood stories of her deceased violinist father.  Her father promises Christine that he will send the \\"Angel of Music\\" to her when he is in Heaven.  Therefore, when an unseen voice begins teaching the young woman, she immediately falls under the Phantom's spell.  What follows is a riveting story in which two different yet ultimately similar individuals embark upon heartbreaking journeys of obsession, shattered innocence, and sacrifice.<br /><br />The cast members of this concert are truly breathtaking.  Ramin Karimloo does superb work as the Phantom.  His voice is mesmerizing in its silken smoothness, yet it can also throb with terrifying anger.  Sierra Boggess was beautiful as Christine; bringing vulnerability and hidden strength to her role as her innocence is lost.  Her rendition of \\"Wishing You Were Somehow Here Again\\" is perhaps the most beautiful and emotionally charged version of this song I have ever heard.  You really get a sense of Christine's longing for her father and for the kind, unseen \\"Angel of music\\" who gave her the gift of song.  Hadley Fraiser is good as Raoul, although no one will ever surpass Steve Barton's gentleness and sincerity.<br />I was equally impressed by the supporting cast.  Most surprising of all, I was impressed with the singer portraying Carlotta.  Usually this character is flamboyant, obnoxious, and extremely unlikable.  However, the woman portraying the diva in this production brings an emotional depth to this role.  I actually felt sorry for Carlotta; a feat not easily accomplished!<br /><br />As a blind individual, I cannot comment on scenery or the costumes.  However, I can say that the renditions of the songs is the best part of this production.  If you are looking for visual spectacle, then know that this production is a concert.  Therefore, the famous \\"chandelier\\" scene, I have been told, is not present.  However, if you want a story with true emotional depth, then consider watching this production. B006VA57CY|5.0|Season one of Downton Abbey set a very high bar. Despite others calling this a 'soap opera', I watched with an open mind and am glad I did. One of my partner's best friends lives in the UK and told us to check this out. I have to say that I was impressed almost instantly. The show is amazingly beautiful. The setting is perfect, the costumes accurate, and the acting superlative.<br /><br />This is not 'The Tudors' or some crap like that (no offense if you enjoy that program, but you probably won't like this program if you enjoy that one). This is stately, slow moving, and interesting social drama from 1910s and 1920s Britain high society. There are some 'plots' among the characters, but nothing too out of the ordinary. The crew did hire a historian to make sure that things were not getting too squirrely.<br /><br />What we see is the inner working of a large estate seen mainly from the servants' points of view. We do get to know the aristocratic family, mainly the eldest daughter and the Lord, but we also get a delightful amount of screen time from an amazing Maggie Smith as the Dowager Countess.<br /><br />There are a lot of British subtleties here, and the first series deals with inheritance laws and such, so those not too familiar might be a little lost at first... but after the first episode that becomes the story in the background and the characters' relationships (friendships most often... very rarely romantic entanglements ensue - which was the norm back then) take center stage. There are a couple of servants who wish to 'move up' the ranks and they hatch some devious plots to discredit some of the more respected servants. The daughters all seem to have their own agenda and the eldest and middle daughter do connive against each other quite a lot with some pretty disastrous results.<br /><br />Season two continues the story, but we are focused less on the inheritance and more on the effects of World War One on the people living and working in Downton. The show delves deeper into individual character's backgrounds and we finally see Vera in this season, who does a good job of becoming an important antagonist.<br /><br />However, there is a little more melodrama and a bit of whining that gets old (looking at you Daisy!). The daughters are all well portrayed and they all seem to have matured in their own way. The eldest daughter was getting on my nerves a little bit in the first half of the first season, but in this season, her character is a delight to watch.<br /><br />There are more surprises in this season, more of those catchy TV series moments that big budget American studios like to throw in... but this doesn't really detract from the overall quality and wonderful acting in this season. Compared to season one it is not quite as good, but compared to dozens of other dramas on television, this is head and shoulders above the rest. And also, this is not BBC, this is Masterpiece Classics. Not BBC.<br /><br />Also of note, the clarity, even on the regular DVD version, is much better in this season. Even my DVD copy looks nearly HD. If I had known that, I would have sprung for the Blu-Ray. 1250006341|5.0|I purchased this for a birthday gift for my partner, she and I are both huge fans of the show.  We were slightly apprehensive about this being more of a 'production' companion; that is, showing more of the filming and set design.  Instead we were pleasantly surprised to find this a delightful companion to the world of Downton Abbey from the era.  Included are many, many gorgeous photos of the abbey and the actors in various scenes.<br /><br />The information included discusses a little about the actual production, but more as the production relates to the actual era and how the filmmakers and designers stayed true to historical aspects.  For example, included is an example of what a person of that era would use to wash their hair, or why a particular costume is the way it is.  There are fun little factoids, such as pointing out that an American heiress in England would likely travel to Paris once a year and spend up to $20,000 (and that is in 1910s currency) on clothing and jewelry to wear for the upcoming year.<br /><br />The reader also finds out that much of the set, props, and costumes are either authentic pieces from the period, or made with pieces from the period.  Many of the costumes have accessories or pieces from the 1920s.  Amazing!  But even those items that had to be made are made with painstaking detail to historical accuracy.<br /><br />And the historical accuracy of the series is what makes this companion book such a welcome addition for any fan.  This book will not tell you of the plot, or spoil any surprises from the show.  It does discuss the characters as if they were real people living in the era (i.e. Cora would have done this...).  But for those interested in the period from the late 1800s through the 1920s of England's high society, this is a wonderful book.  And it really is a welcome gift for fans.<br /><br />*** Of note: the first copy we received had some light damage to a few of the pages (creasing, some folding, and a few tears), amazon shipped a replacement immediately and we are happy now.e are happy now.

0670022950|1.0|I wanted to share a very different point of view on it in a review from the socialist newsweekly The Militant of 02/20/2012/ themilitant.com:<br /><br />Capitalist rule is source of violence, not of its decline<br />Book serves as apology for imperialism's brutal nature<br />(feature article, in review column)<br /><br />BY NAOMI CRAINE<br />The capitalist system--with its state &#34;monopoly on the use of force,&#34; &#34;gentle commerce&#34; and &#34;cosmopolitanism&#34;--has ushered in the least violent epoch in human history. So says Harvard professor Steven Pinker in The Better Angels of Our Nature. Why Violence Has Declined.<br /><br />Pinker's 800-page tome professes to trace through human history a general trend of declining violence--which for him includes everything from wars to eating meat--in order to present the current system of capitalist rule as the highest possible social system giving rise to an increasingly peaceful and progressive world. Among the fundamental problems with the author's historical analysis is that he conspicuously disregards the struggle between exploited and exploiting classes, which Karl Marx explained is the motor force of history. [See [[ASIN:1604880031 The Communist Manifesto]] and [[ASIN:0873486056 Understanding History: Marxist Essays]].<br /><br />Pinker's opening chapters describe past stages of human society that have been overcome, including cannibalism, human sacrifice, chattel slavery, medieval torture devices, and so on. He continues in this vain as he describes inroads in recent decades against racism, discrimination against women, and other forms of oppression.<br /><br />These undeniable advances are above all victories of toiling humanity: our social labor, which has made possible the increase in productivity and development of culture, as well as the struggles of the laboring classes against oppression over millennia of class society.<br /><br />Pinker manages to write a dozen pages under the heading &#34;Civil Rights and the Decline of Lynching and Racial Pogroms&#34; with only a passing mention of the massive, proletarian-led movement that defeated Jim Crow segregation, let alone the battles that preceded and followed it. It's no wonder: the struggle for Black rights was a fight against the state power of the capitalist class, their laws, cops, courts and politicians. A necessary component of it was armed self-defense against state-organized and state-sanctioned violence. And that struggle permanently strengthened the working class and its capacity to effectively fight the bosses and their government. [See [[ASIN:160488021X Malcolm X, Black Liberation, and the Road to Workers Power]].<br /><br />Pinker credits the rise of the state, particularly modern democratic capitalist states in Western Europe and North America, and the ideas of the Enlightenment--the scientific and philosophical ideas that developed as part of the rise of capitalism in the 17th and 18th centuries--for social progress. He praises the &#34;institutions of the Civilizing Process, namely a competent government and police force and a dependable infrastructure for trade and commerce.&#34;<br /><br />The general trend has been a remarkable reduction in violence, he says, spreading &#34;not only downward along the socioeconomic scale but outward across the geographic scale, from a Western European epicenter&#34;--a phrase that captures his anti-working-class and pro-imperialist outlook.<br /><br />The early development of the &#34;institutions of the civilizing process&#34; is explained well in Capital, where Marx describes how capitalism came into being &#34;dripping from head to foot, from every pore, with blood and dirt.&#34; Men, women and children were driven off the land. The &#34;civilizing&#34; anti-vagrancy laws helped drive the dispossessed rural toilers into factories and mines under hours and conditions of labor that exceeded the bounds of human endurance--enforced by the police, judges, jailsand workhouses of the state apparatus.<br /><br />Credit belongs to working class<br /><br />The massive expansion of the hereditary working class on a world scale over the last century, drawing in men and women on every continent, has increased the proletarian habits of mutual trust, tolerance, and class solidarity that working people learn in the course of common struggles. As a result of the accelerated development and struggles by the workers, there is less acceptance today of torture, for instance, and of racist and anti-woman violence. The credit for this however belongs to the revolutionary class in society, the proletariat, not to the capitalist rulers, the source of violence and oppression whose system creates contradictions that invariably explode into war.<br />Pinker goes through some intellectual gymnastics to explain the horrors of the two mass imperialist slaughters of the 20th Century, which would seem to contradict his thesis. He argues that 15 million deaths in World War I and an unprecedented 55 million in World War II are actually not so much--if considered as a percentage of the world's population in contrast to the Mongol Conquests over seven decades in the 13th Century and the fall of the Roman Empire in the 3rd to 5th centuries. He presents numerous charts and graphs to support this quantitative comparison to large-scale massacre and genocide, which many working people will instinctively find morally repugnant. [[Setting the Record Straight on Fascism and World War II in [[ASIN:1604880058 Revolution, Internationalism, and Socialism: The Last Year of Malcolm X (New International no. 14) (New International, Number 14)]]].<br /><br />Pinker conveniently dismisses these world conflagrations as flukes of history, rejecting the likelihood of another war among imperialist powers. He views the increased membership in &#34;intergovernmental organizations&#34; such as the United Nations and European Union, and the increase in so-called peacekeeping missions around the world to be factors limiting the risk of war.<br /><br />`Peacekeepers' tool of imperialism<br /><br />These are in fact some of the main tools used by Washington and other imperialist powers today to maintain their domination of the rest of the world, its resources and labor. It certainly hasn't been a &#34;long peace&#34; for working people in Korea, Vietnam, Congo, Iraq, Afghanistan, Yugoslavia, Haiti and innumerable other countries subjected to imperialist military intervention--often in the name of &#34;peacekeeping&#34; or under the U.N. flag. And as the current world capitalist crisis unfolds, the pressures toward greater conflict--both trade wars and shooting wars--will mount. [See [[ASIN:0873486293 U. S. Hands Off the Mideast!: Cuba Speaks Out at the United Nations]] and [[ASIN:0873487761 The Truth About Yugoslavia: Why Working People Should Oppose Intervention]].<br /><br />As Bolshevik leader V.I. Lenin explained in 1916, &#34;Peaceful alliances [between imperialist powers] prepare the ground for wars, and in their turn grow out of wars.&#34; The &#34;peaceful ... alliance of all the powers for the `pacification' of China&#34; one day shifts to &#34;the non-peaceful conflict of tomorrow, which will prepare the ground for another `peaceful' general alliance for the partition, say, of Turkey, on the day after tomorrow, etc. etc.&#34; The imperialist &#34;civilized hyenas,&#34; as Lenin once called them, haven't changed their spots over the last century.<br /><br />Pinker's bourgeois class bias and contempt for working people is equally on display when he turns his attention to the United States. Under the heading &#34;Recivilization in the 1990s,&#34; he discusses a drop in homicide statistics over the last two decades. Among the factors he credits are that &#34;the Leviathan [the state] got bigger, smarter, and more effective.&#34; Specifically, the incarceration rate multiplied fivefold and the number of cops mushroomed.<br /><br />How &#34;smart&#34; this seems depends on your class perspective. It's working-class neighborhoods, especially Black and Latino communities, that the police target for stop-and-frisk harassment, frame-ups, arbitrary arrests and draconian prison sentences.<br /><br />This is a form of violence that Pinker and his colleagues support, but are embarrassed to acknowledge and never experience for themselves. The Harvard professor is part of a middle class layer that has grown to be millions over the last 20 years--well-placed university professors and administrators, heads of charities and foundations, lawyers and lobbyists--who are largely unconnected to the production, reproduction or circulation of social wealth.<br /><br />This layer, sometimes described as a &#34;meritocracy,&#34; serves as well-paid cheerleaders for the capitalist rulers. They view themselves as smarter and more enlightened, and they fear the working people throughout the world as a &#34;dangerous class.&#34; So they accept, often with mild criticism or feinted regret, that the U.S. must have by far the highest incarceration rate in the world--more than 1 percent of the adult population--as the price of &#34;civilization.&#34;<br /><br />Pinker professes that greater capacity for abstract reasoning, as measured by IQ scores, leads to less violence. Smarter people are more liberal, he argues, more educated, more moral. This smartness has been trickling down to the rest of us unwashed masses--although not as much to the less enlightened &#34;red states&#34; of the South. It's the epitome of a self-serving justification, packaged as scholarly work. [[See [[ASIN:1604880910 The Clinton's Anti-Working-Class Record]] and [[ASIN:1604880872 Are They Rich Because They're Smart?: Class, Privilege and Learning Under Capitalism]]].<br /><br />The development of capitalism has created the productive capacity for the first time ever to meet the material needs of all humanity. The contradiction is that these means are monopolized by a small minority, whose domination is maintained through violence and whose competition and drive for profits lead to the most violent explosions. This contradiction can only be resolved by the working class wresting political power.<br /><br />To get a glimpse of the social forces that actually can put an end to the violent contradictions of capitalism, I recommend reading [[ASIN:0873488296 History of the Russian Revolution]]. And also [[ASIN:1604880368 Women in Cuba: The making of a revolution within the revolution. From Santiago de Cuba and the Rebel Army, to the birth of the Federation of Cuban Women]]. It describes how working people, men and women, built a revolutionary army whose aim was not to kill the enemy, but rather to take power with as little bloodshed as possible. It describes how in the course of the revolutionary war and after its victory the peasants and workers of Cuba increasingly transformed themselves and their society, without preconceived schemes, showing a way forward for humanity.itary intervention--often in the name of \\"peacekeeping\\" or under the U.N. flag. B00000DCH9|5.0|The frenzied but beautiful banjo played by Eric Weissberg on the \\"Piano Man's\\" opener, \\"Travelin' Prayer,\\" sounds like the start of a new, hopeful day. The song is a prayer from Billy Joel to his girlfriend, sung in a rushed, heartfelt way. It's a country-oriented ditty that is a pleasurable mix of fun and worried foresightedness, as Joel lists off the potential hazards that could befall anyone. \\"Travelin' Prayer\\" is a spirited start to a timeless record.<br /><br />Spun incessantly on my parent's turntable, the album \\"Piano Man\\" marked my introduction to music as a youngster (it was released the same year I was born). Its storytelling songs, surprising eclecticism and elegant quality made quite an impression. Hearing the lyrics of \\"Piano Man\\" (the song) may be blas&eacute; for many people these days (due to its constant rotation on the radio and jukeboxes), but it's still a powerful, poignant tune. Like \\"Piano Man,\\" many songs on the rest of the record are narratives that stem from an astute raconteur musician who had already done some serious living by his 20s. Most often Joel's stories on the record relate phases of life, usually through the eyes of the young and invariably depressing -- thankfully periods people grow out of.<br /><br />Billy Joel would rock harder at times later on in his career, but in 1973 he seemed to be aiming for essence and style. Listening to an album like \\"Piano Man\\" takes you back to a time when songs possessed less audio aggression and overly processed elements such as compression. Producer Michael Stewart made an airtight, crisp-sounding record full of dramatic highs and lows in the choruses and an intimate feel. Joel's sensitivity and old-soul tendencies give \\"Piano Man\\" further charm. Joel, of course, is a one-of-a-kind talent who shined on every album of his career in various ways. \\"Piano Man\\" showcases him at the top of his game, particularly side one.<br /><br />Thanks for the rock and roll inauguration, Billy.

0307959856|5.0|P.D. James is a national treasure of Great Britain. The author of more than 20 novels she was named Baroness James of Holland Park in 1991. James was inducted into the International Crime Writing Hall of Fame. Though she is over 90 years old Baroness James has produced an excellent novel in \\"Death Comes to Pemberley. James has long been a fan of Jane Austen (1775-1817) and uses her characters in \\"Pride and Prejudice\\" to tell a tale of dark secrets, secret sex and foul murder set in the Derbyside countryside of 1803.<br />  The Plot: Fitzwilliam Darcy has wed Elizabeth Bennet the fetching hero and heroine from Austen's \\"Pride and Prejudice.\\" It is 1803 and the couple have two male children in the nursery. They reside at Darcy's estate Pemberley. On the night of October 14, 1803 a carriage arrives at the door of the home on the eve of Lady Ann's annual ball. The occupant of the carriage is Lydia Wickham. Lydia is the fatuous younger sister of Elizabeth. In \\"Pride and Prejudice\\" she ran away with the dissolute army officer George Wickham. Lydia reports there has been a murder. Colonel Denny, a friend of her husband, had jumped out of the carriage, entered the forbidding woods and was found dead by George. The man had been beaten to death with a large rock. Wickham is arrested for murder. Darcy, Colonel Fitzwliiam (who is courting Darcy's sister Georgina) and Mr Alverston (who is also courting Georgina) attempt to solve the dastardly murder and free Wickham. Also of importance to the solving of the mystery are members of the Bidwell family. Bidwell lives in the woods being employed as the butler of Pemberley. He has an older daughter living in a distant town; another daughter Louisa (who has romantic and familial secrets) and a dying son named William. His good wife also lives in the cottage in the woods.<br />  Along the way we are reacquainted with Mr. Bingley and his wife Jane another sister of Elizabeth. The novel is expertly written by James. She is able to handle the many charactes and complicated plot with finesse. Her use of the English language is exemplary. One enjoys the deft way she portrays the beauty of the rural landscape of Derbyshire. The scenes set in London's Old Bailey courtroom are tense and exctiing. This humble reviewer marvels how a woman in her 90s could produce a novel whose basis in the immortal classic \\"Pride and Prejudice\\" James has accomplished this assigment with aplomb.  The novel is a story of sin and redemption. It would be an excellent selection for discussion by a book club. I suggest that the group begin with Jane Austen's \\"Pride and Prejudice\\" and then turn to \\"Death Comes to Pemberley.\\" Though you don't have to read PP first it will enrich your enjoyment of the James work.charactes and complicated plot with finesse.
